existing lock, wait and try again\n101: \t\tselect {\n102: \t\tcase <-ctx.Done():\n103: \t\t\t// return the last lock error with the info\n104: \t\t\treturn \"\", err\n105: \t\tcase Operation and Info are required to be set by the caller of Lock.\n116: // Most callers should use NewLockInfo to create a LockInfo value with many\n117: // of the fields populated with suitable default values.\n118: type LockInfo struct {\n119: \t// Unique ID for the lock. NewLockInfo provides a random ID, but this may\n120: \t// be overridden by the lock implementation. The final value of ID will be\n121: \t// returned by the call to Lock.\n122: \tID string\n123: \n124: \t// Terraform operation, provided by the caller.\n125: \tOperation string\n126: \n127: \t// Extra information to store with the lock, provided by the caller.\n128: \tInfo string\n129: \n130: \t// user@hostname when available\n131: \tWho string\n132: \n133: \t// Terraform version\n134: \tVersion string\n135: \n136: \t// Time that the lock was taken.\n137: \tCreated time.Time\n138: \n139: \t// Path to the state file when applicable. err == nil {\n161: \t\tuserName = userInfo.Username\n162: \t}\n163: \thost, _ := os.Hostname()\n164: \n165: \tinfo := &LockInfo{\n166: \t\tID:      id,\n167: \t\tWho:     fmt.Sprintf(\"%s@%s\", userName, host),\n168: \t\tVersion: version.Version,\n169: \t\tCreated: time.Now().UTC(),\n170: \t}\n171: \treturn info\n172: }\n173: \n174: // Err returns the lock info formatted in an error\n175: func (l *LockInfo) Err() error {\n176: \treturn errors.New(l.String())\n177: }\n178: \n179: // Marshal returns a string json representation of the LockInfo\n180: func (l *LockInfo) Marshal() []byte {\n181: \tjs, err := json.Marshal(l)\n182: \tif err != nil {\n183: \t\tpanic(err)\n184: \t}\n185: \treturn js\n186: }\n187: \n188: // String return a multi-line string representation of LockInfo\n189: func (l *LockInfo) String() string {\n190: \ttmpl := `Lock Info:\n191:   ID:        {{.ID}}\n192:   Path:      {{.Path}}\n193:   Operation: {{.Operation}}\n194:   Who:       {{.Who}}\n195:   Version:   {{.Version}}\n196:   Created:   {{.Created}}\n197:   Info:      {{.Info}}\n198: `\n199: \n200: \tt := template.Must(template.New(\"LockInfo\").Parse(tmpl))\n201: \tvar out bytes.Buffer\n202: \tif err := t.Execute(&out, l); err != nil {\n203: \t\tpanic(err)\n204: \t}\n205: \treturn out.String()\n206: }\n207: \n208: // LockError is a specialization of type error that is returned by Locker.Lock\n209: // to indicate that the lock is already held by another process and that\n210: // retrying may be productive to take the lock once the other process releases\n211: // it.\n212: type LockError struct {\n213: \tInfo *LockInfo\n214: \tErr  error\n215: }\n216: \n217: func (e *LockError) Error() string {\n218: \tvar out []string\n219: \tif e.Err != nil {\n220: \t\tout = append(out, e.Err.Error())\n221: \t}\n222: \n223: \tif e.Info != nil {\n224: \t\tout = append(out, e.Info.String())\n225: \t}\n226: \treturn strings.Join(out, \"\\n\")\n227: }\n228: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/terraform--v1.9.0@96263511dba0a473b0636be30c58ee6c489e47ff/-/blob/internal/states/statemgr/locker.go"}}}

historical persistent snapshots, but\n24: // that is an implementation detail and not visible via this API.\n25: type Persistent interface {\n26: \tRefresher\n27: \tPersister\n28: \tOutputReader\n29: }\n30: \n31: // OutputReader is the interface for managers that fetches output values from state\n32: // or another source. This is a refinement of fetching the entire state and digging\n33: // the output values from it because enhanced backends can apply special permissions\n34: // to differentiate reading the state and reading the outputs within the state.\n35: type OutputReader interface {\n36: \t// GetRootOutputValues fetches the root module output values from state or another source\n37: \tGetRootOutputValues(ctx context.Context) (map[string]*states.OutputValue, error)\n38: }\n39: \n40: // Refresher is the interface for managers that can read snapshots from\n41: // persistent storage.\n42: //\n43: // Refresher is usually implemented in conjunction with Reader, with\n44: // RefreshState copying the latest persistent snapshot into the latest\n45: // transient snapshot.\n46: //\n47: // For a type that implements both Refresher and Persister, RefreshState must\n48: // return the result of the most recently completed successful call to\n49: // PersistState, unless another concurrently-running process has persisted\n50: // another snapshot in the mean time.\n51: //\n52: // The Refresher implementation must guarantee that the snapshot is read\n53: // from persistent storage in a way that is safe under concurrent calls to\n54: // PersistState that may be happening in other processes.\n55: type Refresher interface {\n56: \t// RefreshState retrieves a snapshot of state from persistent storage,\n57: \t// returning an error if this is not possible.\n58: \t//\n59: \t// Types that implement RefreshState generally also implement a State\n60: \t// method that returns the result of the latest successful refresh.\n61: \t//\n62: \t// Since only a subset of the data in a state is included when persisting,\n63: \t// a round-trip through PersistState and then RefreshState will often\n64: \t// return only a subset of what was written. Callers must assume that\n65: \t// ephemeral portions of the state may be unpopulated after calling\n66: \t// RefreshState.\n67: \tRefreshState() error\n68: }\n69: \n70: // Persister is the interface for managers that can write snapshots to\n71: // persistent storage.\n72: //\n73: // Persister is usually implemented in conjunction with Writer, with\n74: // PersistState copying the latest transient snapshot to be the new latest\n75: // persistent snapshot.\n76: //\n77: // A Persister implementation must detect updates made by other processes\n78: // that may be running concurrently and avoid destroying those changes. This\n79: // is most commonly achieved by making use of atomic write capabilities on\n80: // the remote storage backend in conjunction with book-keeping with the\n81: // Serial and Lineage fields in the standard state file formats.\n82: //\n83: // Some implementations may optionally utilize config schema to persist\n84: // state. For example, when representing state in an external JSON\n85: // representation.\n86: type Persister interface {\n87: \tPersistState(*schemarepo.Schemas) error\n88: }\n89: \n90: // PersistentMeta is an optional extension to Persistent that allows inspecting\n91: // the metadata associated with the snapshot that was most recently either\n92: // read by RefreshState or written by PersistState.\n93: type PersistentMeta interface {\n94: \t// StateSnapshotMeta returns metadata about the state snapshot most\n95: \t// recently created either by a call to PersistState or read by a call\n96: \t// to RefreshState.\n97: \t//\n98: \t// If no persistent snapshot is yet available in the manager then\n99: \t// the return value is meaningless. This method is primarily available\n100: \t// for testing and logging purposes, and is of little use otherwise.\n101: \tStateSnapshotMeta() SnapshotMeta\n102: }\n103: \n104: // SnapshotMeta contains metadata about a persisted state snapshot.\n105: //\n106: // This metadata is usually (but not necessarily) included as part of the\n107: // \"header\" of a state file, which is then written to a raw blob storage medium\n108: // by a persistent state manager.\n109: //\n110: // Not all state managers will have useful values for all fields in this\n111: // struct, so SnapshotMeta values are of little use beyond testing and logging\n112: // use-cases.\n113: type SnapshotMeta struct {\n114: \t// Lineage and Serial can be used to understand the relationships between\n115: \t// snapshots.\n116: \t//\n117: \t// If two snapshots both have an identical, non-empty Lineage\n118: \t// then the one with the higher Serial is newer than the other.\n119: \t// If the Lineage values are different or empty then the two snapshots\n120: \t// are unrelated and cannot be compared for relative age.\n121: \tLineage string\n122: \tSerial  uint64\n123: \n124: \t// TerraformVersion is the number of the version of Terraform that created\n125: \t// the snapshot.\n126: \tTerraformVersion *version.Version\n127: }\n128: \n129: // IntermediateStateConditionalPersister is an optional extension of\n130: // [Persister] that allows an implementation to tailor the rules for\n131: // whether to create intermediate state snapshots when Terraform Core emits\n132: // events reporting that the state might have changed. This interface is used\n133: // by the local backend when it's been configured to use another backend for\n134: // state storage.\n135: //\n136: // For state managers that don't implement this interface, the local backend's\n137: // StateHook uses a default set of rules that aim to be a good compromise\n138: // between how long a state change can be active before it gets committed as a\n139: // snapshot vs. how many intermediate snapshots will get created. That\n140: // compromise is subject to change over time, but a state manager can implement\n141: // this interface to exert full control over those rules.\n142: type IntermediateStateConditionalPersister interface {\n143: \t// ShouldPersistIntermediateState will be called each time Terraform Core\n144: \t// emits an intermediate state event that is potentially eligible to be\n145: \t// persisted.\n146: \t//\n147: \t// The implemention should return true to signal that the state snapshot\n148: \t// most recently provided to the object's WriteState should be persisted,\n149: \t// or false if it should not be persisted. If this function returns true\n150: \t// then the receiver will see a subsequent call to\n151: \t// [statemgr.Persister.PersistState] to request persistence.\n152: \t//\n153: \t// The implementation must not modify anything reachable through the\n154: \t// arguments, and must not retain pointers to anything reachable through\n155: \t// them after the function returns. However, implementers can assume that\n156: \t// nothing will write to anything reachable through the arguments while\n157: \t// this function is active.\n158: \tShouldPersistIntermediateState(info *IntermediateStatePersistInfo) bool\n159: }\n160: \n161: type IntermediateStatePersistInfo struct {\n162: \t// RequestedPersistInterval is the persist interval requested by whatever\n163: \t// instantiated the StateHook.\n164: \t//\n165: \t// Implementations of [IntermediateStateConditionalPersister] should ideally\n166: \t// respect this, but may ignore it if they use something other than the\n167: \t// passage of time to make their decision.\n168: \tRequestedPersistInterval time.Duration\n169: \n170: \t// LastPersist is the time when the last intermediate state snapshot was\n171: \t// persisted, or the time of the first report for Terraform Core if there\n172: \t// hasn't yet been a persisted snapshot.\n173: \tLastPersist time.Time\n174: \n175: \t// ForcePersist is true when Terraform CLI has receieved an interrupt\n176: \t// signal and is therefore trying to create snapshots more aggressively\n177: \t// in anticipation of possibly being terminated ungracefully.\n178: \t// [IntermediateStateConditionalPersister] implementations should ideally\n179: \t// persist every snapshot they get when this flag is set, unless they have\n180: \t// some external information that implies this shouldn't be necessary.\n181: \tForcePersist bool\n182: }\n183: \n184: // DefaultIntermediateStatePersistRule is the default implementation of\n185: // [IntermediateStateConditionalPersister.ShouldPersistIntermediateState] used\n186: // when the selected state manager doesn't implement that interface.\n187: //\n188: // Implementers of that interface can optionally wrap a call to this function\n189: // if they want to combine the default behavior with some logic of their own.\n190: func DefaultIntermediateStatePersistRule(info *IntermediateStatePersistInfo) bool {\n191: \treturn info.ForcePersist || time.Since(info.LastPersist) >= info.RequestedPersistInterval\n192: }\n193: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/terraform--v1.9.0@96263511dba0a473b0636be30c58ee6c489e47ff/-/blob/internal/states/statemgr/persistent.go"}}}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01T647ZmrqQYUu3sCwkhgFhP","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: # Terraform Core Architecture Summary\\n2: \\n3: This document is a summary of the main components of Terraform Core and how\\n4: data and requests flow between these components. It's intended as a primer\\n5: to help navigate the codebase to dig into more details.\\n6: \\n7: We assume some familiarity with user-facing Terraform concepts like\\n8: configuration, state, CLI workflow, etc. The Terraform website has\\n9: documentation on these ideas.\\n10: \\n11: ## Terraform Request Flow\\n12: \\n13: The following diagram shows an approximation of how a user command is\\n14: executed in Terraform:\\n15: \\n16: ![Terraform Architecture Diagram, described in text below](./images/architecture-overview.png)\\n17: \\n18: Each of the different subsystems (solid boxes) in this diagram is described\\n19: in more detail in a corresponding section below.\\n20: \\n21: ## CLI (`command` package)\\n22: \\n23: Each time a user runs the `terraform` program, aside from some initial\\n24: bootstrapping in the root package (not shown in the diagram) execution\\n25: transfers immediately into one of the \\\"command\\\" implementations in\\n26: [the `command` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/command).\\n27: The mapping between the user-facing command names and\\n28: their corresponding `command` package types can be found in the `commands.go`\\n29: file in the root of the repository.\\n30: \\n31: The full flow illustrated above does not actually apply to _all_ commands,\\n32: but it applies to the main Terraform workflow commands `terraform plan` and\\n33: `terraform apply`, along with a few others.\\n34: \\n35: For these commands, the role of the command implementation is to read and parse\\n36: any command line arguments, command line options, and environment variables\\n37: that are needed for the given command and use them to produce a\\n38: [`backend.Operation`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Operation)\\n39: object that describes an action to be taken.\\n40: \\n41: An _operation_ consists of:\\n42: \\n43: * The action to be taken (e.g. \\\"plan\\\", \\\"apply\\\").\\n44: * The name of the [workspace](https://www.terraform.io/docs/state/workspaces.html)\\n45:   where the action will be taken.\\n46: * Root module input variables to use for the action.\\n47: * For the \\\"plan\\\" operation, a path to the directory containing the configuration's root module.\\n48: * For the \\\"apply\\\" operation, the plan to apply.\\n49: * Various other less-common options/settings such as `-target` addresses, the\\n50: \\\"force\\\" flag, etc.\\n51: \\n52: The operation is then passed to the currently-selected\\n53: [backend](https://www.terraform.io/docs/backends/index.html). Each backend name\\n54: corresponds to an implementation of\\n55: [`backend.Backend`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Backend), using a\\n56: mapping table in\\n57: [the `backend/init` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/init).\\n58: \\n59: Backends that are able to execute operations additionally implement\\n60: [`backend.Enhanced`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Enhanced);\\n61: the command-handling code calls `Operation` with the operation it has\\n62: constructed, and then the backend is responsible for executing that action.\\n63: \\n64: Backends that execute operations, however, do so as an architectural implementation detail and not a\\n65: general feature of backends. That is, the term 'backend' as a Terraform feature is used to refer to\\n66: a plugin that determines where Terraform stores its state snapshots - only the default `local`\\n67: backend and HCP Terraform's backends (`remote`, `cloud`) perform operations.\\n68: \\n69: Thus, most backends do _not_ implement this interface, and so the `command` package wraps these\\n70: backends in an instance of\\n71: [`local.Local`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/local#Local),\\n72: causing the operation to be executed locally within the `terraform` process itself.\\n73: \\n74: ## Backends\\n75: \\n76: A _backend_ determines where Terraform should store its state snapshots.\\n77: \\n78: As described above, the `local` backend also executes operations on behalf of most other\\n79: backends. It uses a _state manager_\\n80: (either\\n81: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) if the\\n82: local backend is being used directly, or an implementation provided by whatever\\n83: backend is being wrapped) to retrieve the current state for the workspace\\n84: specified in the operation, then uses the _config loader_ to load and do\\n85: initial processing/validation of the configuration specified in the\\n86: operation. It then uses these, along with the other settings given in the\\n87: operation, to construct a\\n88: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context),\\n89: which is the main object that actually performs Terraform operations.\\n90: \\n91: The `local` backend finally calls an appropriate method on that context to\\n92: begin execution of the relevant command, such as\\n93: [`Plan`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context.Plan)\\n94: or\\n95: [`Apply`](), which in turn constructs a graph using a _graph builder_,\\n96: described in a later section.\\n97: \\n98: ## Configuration Loader\\n99: \\n100: The top-level configuration structure is represented by model types in\\n101: [package `configs`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs).\\n102: A whole configuration (the root module plus all of its descendent modules)\\n103: is represented by\\n104: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config).\\n105: \\n106: The `configs` package contains some low-level functionality for constructing\\n107: configuration objects, but the main entry point is in the sub-package\\n108: [`configload`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload]),\\n109: via\\n110: [`configload.Loader`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload#Loader).\\n111: A loader deals with all of the details of installing child modules\\n112: (during `terraform init`) and then locating those modules again when a\\n113: configuration is loaded by a backend. It takes the path to a root module\\n114: and recursively loads all of the child modules to produce a single\\n115: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config)\\n116: representing the entire configuration.\\n117: \\n118: Terraform expects configuration files written in the Terraform language, which\\n119: is a DSL built on top of\\n120: [HCL](https://github.com/hashicorp/hcl). Some parts of the configuration\\n121: cannot be interpreted until we build and walk the graph, since they depend\\n122: on the outcome of other parts of the configuration, and so these parts of\\n123: the configuration remain represented as the low-level HCL types\\n124: [`hcl.Body`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Body)\\n125: and\\n126: [`hcl.Expression`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Expression),\\n127: allowing Terraform to interpret them at a more appropriate time.\\n128: \\n129: ## State Manager\\n130: \\n131: A _state manager_ is responsible for storing and retrieving snapshots of the\\n132: [Terraform state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/index.html)\\n133: for a particular workspace. Each manager is an implementation of\\n134: some combination of interfaces in\\n135: [the `statemgr` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr),\\n136: with most practical managers implementing the full set of operations\\n137: described by\\n138: [`statemgr.Full`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Full)\\n139: provided by a _backend_. The smaller interfaces exist primarily for use in\\n140: other function signatures to be explicit about what actions the function might\\n141: take on the state manager; there is little reason to write a state manager\\n142: that does not implement all of `statemgr.Full`.\\n143: \\n144: The implementation\\n145: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) is used\\n146: by default (by the `local` backend) and is responsible for the familiar\\n147: `terraform.tfstate` local file that most Terraform users start with, before\\n148: they switch to [remote state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/remote.html).\\n149: Other implementations of `statemgr.Full` are used to implement remote state.\\n150: Each of these saves and retrieves state via a remote network service\\n151: appropriate to the backend that creates it.\\n152: \\n153: A state manager accepts and returns a state snapshot as a\\n154: [`states.State`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#State)\\n155: object. The state manager is responsible for exactly how that object is\\n156: serialized and stored, but all state managers at the time of writing use\\n157: the same JSON serialization format, storing the resulting JSON bytes in some\\n158: kind of arbitrary blob store.\\n159: \\n160: ## Graph Builder\\n161: \\n162: A _graph builder_ is called by a\\n163: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context)\\n164: method (e.g. `Plan` or `Apply`) to produce the graph that will be used\\n165: to represent the necessary steps for that operation and the dependency\\n166: relationships between them.\\n167: \\n168: In most cases, the\\n169: [vertices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_(graph_theory)) of Terraform's\\n170: graphs each represent a specific object in the configuration, or something\\n171: derived from those configuration objects. For example, each `resource` block\\n172: in the configuration has one corresponding\\n173: [`GraphNodeConfigResource`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeConfigResource)\\n174: vertex representing it in the \\\"plan\\\" graph. (Terraform Core uses terminology\\n175: inconsistently, describing graph _vertices_ also as graph _nodes_ in various\\n176: places. These both describe the same concept.)\\n177: \\n178: The [edges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_graph_theory_terms#edge)\\n179: in the graph represent \\\"must happen after\\\" relationships. These define the\\n180: order in which the vertices are evaluated, ensuring that e.g. one resource is\\n181: created before another resource that depends on it.\\n182: \\n183: Each operation has its own graph builder, because the graph building process\\n184: is different for each. For example, a \\\"plan\\\" operation needs a graph built\\n185: directly from the configuration, but an \\\"apply\\\" operation instead builds its\\n186: graph from the set of changes described in the plan that is being applied.\\n187: \\n188: The graph builders all work in terms of a sequence of _transforms_, which\\n189: are implementations of\\n190: [`terraform.GraphTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphTransformer).\\n191: Implementations of this interface just take a graph and mutate it in any\\n192: way needed, and so the set of available transforms is quite varied. Some\\n193: important examples include:\\n194: \\n195: * [`ConfigTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ConfigTransformer),\\n196:   which creates a graph vertex for each `resource` block in the configuration.\\n197: \\n198: * [`StateTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#StateTransformer),\\n199:   which creates a graph vertex for each resource instance currently tracked\\n200:   in the state.\\n201: \\n202: * [`ReferenceTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ReferenceTransformer),\\n203:   which analyses the configuration to find dependencies between resources and\\n204:   other objects and creates any necessary \\\"happens after\\\" edges for these.\\n205: \\n206: * [`ProviderTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ProviderTransformer),\\n207:   which associates each resource or resource instance with exactly one\\n208:   provider configuration (implementing\\n209:   [the inheritance rules](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/modules/develop/providers.html))\\n210:   and then creates \\\"happens after\\\" edges to ensure that the providers are\\n211:   initialized before taking any actions with the resources that belong to\\n212:   them.\\n213: \\n214: There are many more different graph transforms, which can be discovered\\n215: by reading the source code for the different graph builders. Each graph\\n216: builder uses a different subset of these depending on the needs of the\\n217: operation that is being performed.\\n218: \\n219: The result of graph building is a\\n220: [`terraform.Graph`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Graph), which\\n221: can then be processed using a _graph walker_.\\n222: \\n223: ## Graph Walk\\n224: \\n225: The process of walking the graph visits each vertex of that graph in a way\\n226: which respects the \\\"happens after\\\" edges in the graph. The walk algorithm\\n227: itself is implemented in\\n228: [the low-level `dag` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk)\\n229: (where \\\"DAG\\\" is short for [_Directed Acyclic Graph_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Directed_acyclic_graph)), in\\n230: [`AcyclicGraph.Walk`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk).\\n231: However, the \\\"interesting\\\" Terraform walk functionality is implemented in\\n232: [`terraform.ContextGraphWalker`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ContextGraphWalker),\\n233: which implements a small set of higher-level operations that are performed\\n234: during the graph walk:\\n235: \\n236: * `EnterPath` is called once for each module in the configuration, taking a\\n237:   module address and returning a\\n238:   [`terraform.EvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#EvalContext)\\n239:   that tracks objects within that module. `terraform.Context` is the _global_\\n240:   context for the entire operation, while `terraform.EvalContext` is a\\n241:   context for processing within a single module, and is the primary means\\n242:   by which the namespaces in each module are kept separate.\\n243: \\n244: Each vertex in the graph is evaluated, in an order that guarantees that the\\n245: \\\"happens after\\\" edges will be respected. If possible, the graph walk algorithm\\n246: will evaluate multiple vertices concurrently. Vertex evaluation code must\\n247: therefore make careful use of concurrency primitives such as mutexes in order\\n248: to coordinate access to shared objects such as the `states.State` object.\\n249: In most cases, we use the helper wrapper\\n250: [`states.SyncState`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#SyncState)\\n251: to safely implement concurrent reads and writes from the shared state.\\n252: \\n253: ## Vertex Evaluation\\n254: \\n255: The action taken for each vertex during the graph walk is called\\n256: _execution_. Execution runs a sequence of arbitrary actions that make sense\\n257: for a particular vertex type.\\n258: \\n259: For example, evaluation of a vertex representing a resource instance during\\n260: a plan operation would include the following high-level steps:\\n261: \\n262: * Retrieve the resource's associated provider from the `EvalContext`. This\\n263:   should already be initialized earlier by the provider's own graph vertex,\\n264:   due to the \\\"happens after\\\" edge between the resource node and the provider\\n265:   node.\\n266: \\n267: * Retrieve from the state the portion relevant to the specific resource\\n268:   instance being evaluated.\\n269: \\n270: * Evaluate the attribute expressions given for the resource in configuration.\\n271:   This often involves retrieving the state of _other_ resource instances so\\n272:   that their values can be copied or transformed into the current instance's\\n273:   attributes, which is coordinated by the `EvalContext`.\\n274: \\n275: * Pass the current instance state and the resource configuration to the\\n276:   provider, asking the provider to produce an _instance diff_ representing the\\n277:   differences between the state and the configuration.\\n278: \\n279: * Save the instance diff as part of the plan that is being constructed by\\n280:   this operation.\\n281: \\n282: Each execution step for a vertex is an implementation of\\n283: [`terraform.Execute`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Execute).\\n284: As with graph transforms, the behavior of these implementations varies widely:\\n285: whereas graph transforms can take any action against the graph, an `Execute`\\n286: implementation can take any action against the `EvalContext`.\\n287: \\n288: The implementation of `terraform.EvalContext` used in real processing\\n289: (as opposed to testing) is\\n290: [`terraform.BuiltinEvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#BuiltinEvalContext).\\n291: It provides coordinated access to plugins, the current state, and the current\\n292: plan via the `EvalContext` interface methods.\\n293: \\n294: In order to be executed, a vertex must implement\\n295: [`terraform.GraphNodeExecutable`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeExecutable),\\n296: which has a single `Execute` method that handles. There are numerous `Execute`\\n297: implementations with different behaviors, but some prominent examples are:\\n298: \\n299: * [NodePlannableResource.Execute](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#NodePlannableResourceInstance.Execute), which handles the `plan` operation.\\n300: \\n301: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/terraform--v1.9.0@96263511dba0a473b0636be30c58ee6c489e47ff/-/blob/docs/architecture.md%3FL1-300\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"83859470-86b9-4447-9f20-18b088f27763","uuid":"bc86ad01-770f-464b-96b0-957921004ea3","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: # Terraform Core Architecture Summary\\n2: \\n3: This document is a summary of the main components of Terraform Core and how\\n4: data and requests flow between these components. CLI (`command` package)\\n22: \\n23: Each time a user runs the `terraform` program, aside from some initial\\n24: bootstrapping in the root package (not shown in the diagram) execution\\n25: transfers immediately into one of the \\\"command\\\" implementations in\\n26: [the `command` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/command).\\n27: The mapping between the user-facing command names and\\n28: their corresponding `command` package types can be found in the `commands.go`\\n29: file in the root of the repository.\\n30: \\n31: The full flow illustrated above does not actually apply to _all_ commands,\\n32: but it applies to the main Terraform workflow commands `terraform plan` and\\n33: `terraform apply`, along with a few others.\\n34: \\n35: For these commands, the role of the command implementation is to read and parse\\n36: any command line arguments, command line options, and environment variables\\n37: that are needed for the given command and use them to produce a\\n38: [`backend.Operation`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Operation)\\n39: object that describes an action to be taken.\\n40: \\n41: An _operation_ consists of:\\n42: \\n43: * The action to be taken (e.g. \\\"plan\\\", \\\"apply\\\").\\n44: * The name of the [workspace](https://www.terraform.io/docs/state/workspaces.html)\\n45:   where the action will be taken.\\n46: * Root module input variables to use for the action.\\n47: * For the \\\"plan\\\" operation, a path to the directory containing the configuration's root module.\\n48: * For the \\\"apply\\\" operation, the plan to apply.\\n49: * Various other less-common options/settings such as `-target` addresses, the\\n50: \\\"force\\\" flag, etc.\\n51: \\n52: The operation is then passed to the currently-selected\\n53: [backend](https://www.terraform.io/docs/backends/index.html). Each backend name\\n54: corresponds to an implementation of\\n55: [`backend.Backend`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Backend), using a\\n56: mapping table in\\n57: [the `backend/init` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/init).\\n58: \\n59: Backends that are able to execute operations additionally implement\\n60: [`backend.Enhanced`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Enhanced);\\n61: the command-handling code calls `Operation` with the operation it has\\n62: constructed, and then the backend is responsible for executing that action.\\n63: \\n64: Backends that execute operations, however, do so as an architectural implementation detail and not a\\n65: general feature of backends. That is, the term 'backend' as a Terraform feature is used to refer to\\n66: a plugin that determines where Terraform stores its state snapshots - only the default `local`\\n67: backend and HCP Terraform's backends (`remote`, `cloud`) perform operations.\\n68: \\n69: Thus, most backends do _not_ implement this interface, and so the `command` package wraps these\\n70: backends in an instance of\\n71: [`local.Local`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/local#Local),\\n72: causing the operation to be executed locally within the `terraform` process itself.\\n73: \\n74: ## Backends\\n75: \\n76: A _backend_ determines where Terraform should store its state snapshots.\\n77: \\n78: As described above, the `local` backend also executes operations on behalf of most other\\n79: backends. It uses a _state manager_\\n80: (either\\n81: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) if the\\n82: local backend is being used directly, or an implementation provided by whatever\\n83: backend is being wrapped) to retrieve the current state for the workspace\\n84: specified in the operation, then uses the _config loader_ to load and do\\n85: initial processing/validation of the configuration specified in the\\n86: operation. It then uses these, along with the other settings given in the\\n87: operation, to construct a\\n88: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context),\\n89: which is the main object that actually performs Terraform operations.\\n90: \\n91: The `local` backend finally calls an appropriate method on that context to\\n92: begin execution of the relevant command, such as\\n93: [`Plan`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context.Plan)\\n94: or\\n95: [`Apply`](), which in turn constructs a graph using a _graph builder_,\\n96: described in a later section.\\n97: \\n98: ## Configuration Loader\\n99: \\n100: The top-level configuration structure is represented by model types in\\n101: [package `configs`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs).\\n102: A whole configuration (the root module plus all of its descendent modules)\\n103: is represented by\\n104: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config).\\n105: \\n106: The `configs` package contains some low-level functionality for constructing\\n107: configuration objects, but the main entry point is in the sub-package\\n108: [`configload`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload]),\\n109: via\\n110: [`configload.Loader`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload#Loader).\\n111: A loader deals with all of the details of installing child modules\\n112: (during `terraform init`) and then locating those modules again when a\\n113: configuration is loaded by a backend. It takes the path to a root module\\n114: and recursively loads all of the child modules to produce a single\\n115: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config)\\n116: representing the entire configuration.\\n117: \\n118: Terraform expects configuration files written in the Terraform language, which\\n119: is a DSL built on top of\\n120: [HCL](https://github.com/hashicorp/hcl). Some parts of the configuration\\n121: cannot be interpreted until we build and walk the graph, since they depend\\n122: on the outcome of other parts of the configuration, and so these parts of\\n123: the configuration remain represented as the low-level HCL types\\n124: [`hcl.Body`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Body)\\n125: and\\n126: [`hcl.Expression`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Expression),\\n127: allowing Terraform to interpret them at a more appropriate time.\\n128: \\n129: ## State Manager\\n130: \\n131: A _state manager_ is responsible for storing and retrieving snapshots of the\\n132: [Terraform state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/index.html)\\n133: for a particular workspace. Each manager is an implementation of\\n134: some combination of interfaces in\\n135: [the `statemgr` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr),\\n136: with most practical managers implementing the full set of operations\\n137: described by\\n138: [`statemgr.Full`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Full)\\n139: provided by a _backend_. The smaller interfaces exist primarily for use in\\n140: other function signatures to be explicit about what actions the function might\\n141: take on the state manager; there is little reason to write a state manager\\n142: that does not implement all of `statemgr.Full`.\\n143: \\n144: The implementation\\n145: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) is used\\n146: by default (by the `local` backend) and is responsible for the familiar\\n147: `terraform.tfstate` local file that most Terraform users start with, before\\n148: they switch to [remote state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/remote.html).\\n149: Other implementations of `statemgr.Full` are used to implement remote state.\\n150: Each of these saves and retrieves state via a remote network service\\n151: appropriate to the backend that creates it.\\n152: \\n153: A state manager accepts and returns a state snapshot as a\\n154: [`states.State`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#State)\\n155: object. The state manager is responsible for exactly how that object is\\n156: serialized and stored, but all state managers at the time of writing use\\n157: the same JSON serialization format, storing the resulting JSON bytes in some\\n158: kind of arbitrary blob store.\\n159: \\n160: ## Graph Builder\\n161: \\n162: A _graph builder_ is called by a\\n163: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context)\\n164: method (e.g. `Plan` or `Apply`) to produce the graph that will be used\\n165: to represent the necessary steps for that operation and the dependency\\n166: relationships between them.\\n167: \\n168: In most cases, the\\n169: [vertices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_(graph_theory)) of Terraform's\\n170: graphs each represent a specific object in the configuration, or something\\n171: derived from those configuration objects. For example, each `resource` block\\n172: in the configuration has one corresponding\\n173: [`GraphNodeConfigResource`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeConfigResource)\\n174: vertex representing it in the \\\"plan\\\" graph. (Terraform Core uses terminology\\n175: inconsistently, describing graph _vertices_ also as graph _nodes_ in various\\n176: places. These both describe the same concept.)\\n177: \\n178: The [edges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_graph_theory_terms#edge)\\n179: in the graph represent \\\"must happen after\\\" relationships. These define the\\n180: order in which the vertices are evaluated, ensuring that e.g. one resource is\\n181: created before another resource that depends on it.\\n182: \\n183: Each operation has its own graph builder, because the graph building process\\n184: is different for each. For example, a \\\"plan\\\" operation needs a graph built\\n185: directly from the configuration, but an \\\"apply\\\" operation instead builds its\\n186: graph from the set of changes described in the plan that is being applied.\\n187: \\n188: The graph builders all work in terms of a sequence of _transforms_, which\\n189: are implementations of\\n190: [`terraform.GraphTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphTransformer).\\n191: Implementations of this interface just take a graph and mutate it in any\\n192: way needed, and so the set of available transforms is quite varied. Some\\n193: important examples include:\\n194: \\n195: * [`ConfigTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ConfigTransformer),\\n196:   which creates a graph vertex for each `resource` block in the configuration.\\n197: \\n198: * [`StateTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#StateTransformer),\\n199:   which creates a graph vertex for each resource instance currently tracked\\n200:   in the state.\\n201: \\n202: * [`ReferenceTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ReferenceTransformer),\\n203:   which analyses the configuration to find dependencies between resources and\\n204:   other objects and creates any necessary \\\"happens after\\\" edges for these.\\n205: \\n206: * [`ProviderTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ProviderTransformer),\\n207:   which associates each resource or resource instance with exactly one\\n208:   provider configuration (implementing\\n209:   [the inheritance rules](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/modules/develop/providers.html))\\n210:   and then creates \\\"happens after\\\" edges to ensure that the providers are\\n211:   initialized before taking any actions with the resources that belong to\\n212:   them.\\n213: \\n214: There are many more different graph transforms, which can be discovered\\n215: by reading the source code for the different graph builders. Each graph\\n216: builder uses a different subset of these depending on the needs of the\\n217: operation that is being performed.\\n218: \\n219: The result of graph building is a\\n220: [`terraform.Graph`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Graph), which\\n221: can then be processed using a _graph walker_.\\n222: \\n223: ## Graph Walk\\n224: \\n225: The process of walking the graph visits each vertex of that graph in a way\\n226: which respects the \\\"happens after\\\" edges in the graph. The walk algorithm\\n227: itself is implemented in\\n228: [the low-level `dag` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk)\\n229: (where \\\"DAG\\\" is short for [_Directed Acyclic Graph_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Directed_acyclic_graph)), in\\n230: [`AcyclicGraph.Walk`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk).\\n231: However, the \\\"interesting\\\" Terraform walk functionality is implemented in\\n232: [`terraform.ContextGraphWalker`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ContextGraphWalker),\\n233: which implements a small set of higher-level operations that are performed\\n234: during the graph walk:\\n235: \\n236: * `EnterPath` is called once for each module in the configuration, taking a\\n237:   module address and returning a\\n238:   [`terraform.EvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#EvalContext)\\n239:   that tracks objects within that module. `terraform.Context` is the _global_\\n240:   context for the entire operation, while `terraform.EvalContext` is a\\n241:   context for processing within a single module, and is the primary means\\n242:   by which the namespaces in each module are kept separate.\\n243: \\n244: Each vertex in the graph is evaluated, in an order that guarantees that the\\n245: \\\"happens after\\\" edges will be respected. If possible, the graph walk algorithm\\n246: will evaluate multiple vertices concurrently. Vertex evaluation code must\\n247: therefore make careful use of concurrency primitives such as mutexes in order\\n248: to coordinate access to shared objects such as the `states.State` object.\\n249: In most cases, we use the helper wrapper\\n250: [`states.SyncState`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#SyncState)\\n251: to safely implement concurrent reads and writes from the shared state.\\n252: \\n253: ## Vertex Evaluation\\n254: \\n255: The action taken for each vertex during the graph walk is called\\n256: _execution_. Execution runs a sequence of arbitrary actions that make sense\\n257: for a particular vertex type.\\n258: \\n259: For example, evaluation of a vertex representing a resource instance during\\n260: a plan operation would include the following high-level steps:\\n261: \\n262: * Retrieve the resource's associated provider from the `EvalContext`. This\\n263:   should already be initialized earlier by the provider's own graph vertex,\\n264:   due to the \\\"happens after\\\" edge between the resource node and the provider\\n265:   node.\\n266: \\n267: * Retrieve from the state the portion relevant to the specific resource\\n268:   instance being evaluated.\\n269: \\n270: * Evaluate the attribute expressions given for the resource in configuration.\\n271:   This often involves retrieving the state of _other_ resource instances so\\n272:   that their values can be copied or transformed into the current instance's\\n273:   attributes, which is coordinated by the `EvalContext`.\\n274: \\n275: * Pass the current instance state and the resource configuration to the\\n276:   provider, asking the provider to produce an _instance diff_ representing the\\n277:   differences between the state and the configuration.\\n278: \\n279: * Save the instance diff as part of the plan that is being constructed by\\n280:   this operation.\\n281: \\n282: Each execution step for a vertex is an implementation of\\n283: [`terraform.Execute`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Execute).\\n284: As with graph transforms, the behavior of these implementations varies widely:\\n285: whereas graph transforms can take any action against the graph, an `Execute`\\n286: implementation can take any action against the `EvalContext`.\\n287: \\n288: The implementation of `terraform.EvalContext` used in real processing\\n289: (as opposed to testing) is\\n290: [`terraform.BuiltinEvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#BuiltinEvalContext).\\n291: It provides coordinated access to plugins, the current state, and the current\\n292: plan via the `EvalContext` interface methods.\\n293: \\n294: In order to be executed, a vertex must implement\\n295: [`terraform.GraphNodeExecutable`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeExecutable),\\n296: which has a single `Execute` method that handles. There are numerous `Execute`\\n297: implementations with different behaviors, but some prominent examples are:\\n298: \\n299: * [NodePlannableResource.Execute](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#NodePlannableResourceInstance.Execute), which handles the `plan` operation.\\n300: \\n301: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/terraform--v1.9.0@96263511dba0a473b0636be30c58ee6c489e47ff/-/blob/docs/architecture.md%3FL1-300\"}","structuredContent":{"content":"1: # Terraform Core Architecture Summary\n2: \n3: This document is a summary of the main components of Terraform Core and how\n4: data and requests flow between these components. It's intended as a primer\n5: to help navigate the codebase to dig into more details.\n6: \n7: We assume some familiarity with user-facing Terraform concepts like\n8: configuration, state, CLI workflow, etc. The Terraform website has\n9: documentation on these ideas.\n10: \n11: ## Terraform Request Flow\n12: \n13: The following diagram shows an approximation of how a user command is\n14: executed in Terraform:\n15: \n16: ![Terraform Architecture Diagram, described in text below](./images/architecture-overview.png)\n17: \n18: Each of the different subsystems (solid boxes) in this diagram is described\n19: in more detail in a corresponding section below.\n20: \n21: ## CLI (`command` package)\n22: \n23: Each time a user runs the `terraform` program, aside from some initial\n24: bootstrapping in the root package (not shown in the diagram) execution\n25: transfers immediately into one of the \"command\" implementations in\n26: [the `command` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/command).\n27: The mapping between the user-facing command names and\n28: their corresponding `command` package types can be found in the `commands.go`\n29: file in the root of the repository.\n30: \n31: The full flow illustrated above does not actually apply to _all_ commands,\n32: but it applies to the main Terraform workflow commands `terraform plan` and\n33: `terraform apply`, along with a few others.\n34: \n35: For these commands, the role of the command implementation is to read and parse\n36: any command line arguments, command line options, and environment variables\n37: that are needed for the given command and use them to produce a\n38: [`backend.Operation`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Operation)\n39: object that describes an action to be taken.\n40: \n41: An _operation_ consists of:\n42: \n43: * The action to be taken (e.g. \"plan\", \"apply\").\n44: * The name of the [workspace](https://www.terraform.io/docs/state/workspaces.html)\n45:   where the action will be taken.\n46: * Root module input variables to use for the action.\n47: * For the \"plan\" operation, a path to the directory containing the configuration's root module.\n48: * For the \"apply\" operation, the plan to apply.\n49: * Various other less-common options/settings such as `-target` addresses, the\n50: \"force\" flag, etc.\n51: \n52: The operation is then passed to the currently-selected\n53: [backend](https://www.terraform.io/docs/backends/index.html). Each backend name\n54: corresponds to an implementation of\n55: [`backend.Backend`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Backend), using a\n56: mapping table in\n57: [the `backend/init` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/init).\n58: \n59: Backends that are able to execute operations additionally implement\n60: [`backend.Enhanced`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend#Enhanced);\n61: the command-handling code calls `Operation` with the operation it has\n62: constructed, and then the backend is responsible for executing that action.\n63: \n64: Backends that execute operations, however, do so as an architectural implementation detail and not a\n65: general feature of backends. That is, the term 'backend' as a Terraform feature is used to refer to\n66: a plugin that determines where Terraform stores its state snapshots - only the default `local`\n67: backend and HCP Terraform's backends (`remote`, `cloud`) perform operations.\n68: \n69: Thus, most backends do _not_ implement this interface, and so the `command` package wraps these\n70: backends in an instance of\n71: [`local.Local`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/backend/local#Local),\n72: causing the operation to be executed locally within the `terraform` process itself.\n73: \n74: ## Backends\n75: \n76: A _backend_ determines where Terraform should store its state snapshots.\n77: \n78: As described above, the `local` backend also executes operations on behalf of most other\n79: backends. It uses a _state manager_\n80: (either\n81: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) if the\n82: local backend is being used directly, or an implementation provided by whatever\n83: backend is being wrapped) to retrieve the current state for the workspace\n84: specified in the operation, then uses the _config loader_ to load and do\n85: initial processing/validation of the configuration specified in the\n86: operation. It then uses these, along with the other settings given in the\n87: operation, to construct a\n88: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context),\n89: which is the main object that actually performs Terraform operations.\n90: \n91: The `local` backend finally calls an appropriate method on that context to\n92: begin execution of the relevant command, such as\n93: [`Plan`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context.Plan)\n94: or\n95: [`Apply`](), which in turn constructs a graph using a _graph builder_,\n96: described in a later section.\n97: \n98: ## Configuration Loader\n99: \n100: The top-level configuration structure is represented by model types in\n101: [package `configs`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs).\n102: A whole configuration (the root module plus all of its descendent modules)\n103: is represented by\n104: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config).\n105: \n106: The `configs` package contains some low-level functionality for constructing\n107: configuration objects, but the main entry point is in the sub-package\n108: [`configload`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload]),\n109: via\n110: [`configload.Loader`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs/configload#Loader).\n111: A loader deals with all of the details of installing child modules\n112: (during `terraform init`) and then locating those modules again when a\n113: configuration is loaded by a backend. It takes the path to a root module\n114: and recursively loads all of the child modules to produce a single\n115: [`configs.Config`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/configs#Config)\n116: representing the entire configuration.\n117: \n118: Terraform expects configuration files written in the Terraform language, which\n119: is a DSL built on top of\n120: [HCL](https://github.com/hashicorp/hcl). Some parts of the configuration\n121: cannot be interpreted until we build and walk the graph, since they depend\n122: on the outcome of other parts of the configuration, and so these parts of\n123: the configuration remain represented as the low-level HCL types\n124: [`hcl.Body`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Body)\n125: and\n126: [`hcl.Expression`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/hcl/v2/#Expression),\n127: allowing Terraform to interpret them at a more appropriate time.\n128: \n129: ## State Manager\n130: \n131: A _state manager_ is responsible for storing and retrieving snapshots of the\n132: [Terraform state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/index.html)\n133: for a particular workspace. Each manager is an implementation of\n134: some combination of interfaces in\n135: [the `statemgr` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr),\n136: with most practical managers implementing the full set of operations\n137: described by\n138: [`statemgr.Full`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Full)\n139: provided by a _backend_. The smaller interfaces exist primarily for use in\n140: other function signatures to be explicit about what actions the function might\n141: take on the state manager; there is little reason to write a state manager\n142: that does not implement all of `statemgr.Full`.\n143: \n144: The implementation\n145: [`statemgr.Filesystem`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states/statemgr#Filesystem) is used\n146: by default (by the `local` backend) and is responsible for the familiar\n147: `terraform.tfstate` local file that most Terraform users start with, before\n148: they switch to [remote state](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/state/remote.html).\n149: Other implementations of `statemgr.Full` are used to implement remote state.\n150: Each of these saves and retrieves state via a remote network service\n151: appropriate to the backend that creates it.\n152: \n153: A state manager accepts and returns a state snapshot as a\n154: [`states.State`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#State)\n155: object. The state manager is responsible for exactly how that object is\n156: serialized and stored, but all state managers at the time of writing use\n157: the same JSON serialization format, storing the resulting JSON bytes in some\n158: kind of arbitrary blob store.\n159: \n160: ## Graph Builder\n161: \n162: A _graph builder_ is called by a\n163: [`terraform.Context`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Context)\n164: method (e.g. `Plan` or `Apply`) to produce the graph that will be used\n165: to represent the necessary steps for that operation and the dependency\n166: relationships between them.\n167: \n168: In most cases, the\n169: [vertices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_(graph_theory)) of Terraform's\n170: graphs each represent a specific object in the configuration, or something\n171: derived from those configuration objects. For example, each `resource` block\n172: in the configuration has one corresponding\n173: [`GraphNodeConfigResource`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeConfigResource)\n174: vertex representing it in the \"plan\" graph. (Terraform Core uses terminology\n175: inconsistently, describing graph _vertices_ also as graph _nodes_ in various\n176: places. These both describe the same concept.)\n177: \n178: The [edges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_graph_theory_terms#edge)\n179: in the graph represent \"must happen after\" relationships. These define the\n180: order in which the vertices are evaluated, ensuring that e.g. one resource is\n181: created before another resource that depends on it.\n182: \n183: Each operation has its own graph builder, because the graph building process\n184: is different for each. For example, a \"plan\" operation needs a graph built\n185: directly from the configuration, but an \"apply\" operation instead builds its\n186: graph from the set of changes described in the plan that is being applied.\n187: \n188: The graph builders all work in terms of a sequence of _transforms_, which\n189: are implementations of\n190: [`terraform.GraphTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphTransformer).\n191: Implementations of this interface just take a graph and mutate it in any\n192: way needed, and so the set of available transforms is quite varied. Some\n193: important examples include:\n194: \n195: * [`ConfigTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ConfigTransformer),\n196:   which creates a graph vertex for each `resource` block in the configuration.\n197: \n198: * [`StateTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#StateTransformer),\n199:   which creates a graph vertex for each resource instance currently tracked\n200:   in the state.\n201: \n202: * [`ReferenceTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ReferenceTransformer),\n203:   which analyses the configuration to find dependencies between resources and\n204:   other objects and creates any necessary \"happens after\" edges for these.\n205: \n206: * [`ProviderTransformer`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ProviderTransformer),\n207:   which associates each resource or resource instance with exactly one\n208:   provider configuration (implementing\n209:   [the inheritance rules](https://www.terraform.io/docs/language/modules/develop/providers.html))\n210:   and then creates \"happens after\" edges to ensure that the providers are\n211:   initialized before taking any actions with the resources that belong to\n212:   them.\n213: \n214: There are many more different graph transforms, which can be discovered\n215: by reading the source code for the different graph builders. Each graph\n216: builder uses a different subset of these depending on the needs of the\n217: operation that is being performed.\n218: \n219: The result of graph building is a\n220: [`terraform.Graph`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Graph), which\n221: can then be processed using a _graph walker_.\n222: \n223: ## Graph Walk\n224: \n225: The process of walking the graph visits each vertex of that graph in a way\n226: which respects the \"happens after\" edges in the graph. The walk algorithm\n227: itself is implemented in\n228: [the low-level `dag` package](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk)\n229: (where \"DAG\" is short for [_Directed Acyclic Graph_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Directed_acyclic_graph)), in\n230: [`AcyclicGraph.Walk`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/dag#AcyclicGraph.Walk).\n231: However, the \"interesting\" Terraform walk functionality is implemented in\n232: [`terraform.ContextGraphWalker`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#ContextGraphWalker),\n233: which implements a small set of higher-level operations that are performed\n234: during the graph walk:\n235: \n236: * `EnterPath` is called once for each module in the configuration, taking a\n237:   module address and returning a\n238:   [`terraform.EvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#EvalContext)\n239:   that tracks objects within that module. `terraform.Context` is the _global_\n240:   context for the entire operation, while `terraform.EvalContext` is a\n241:   context for processing within a single module, and is the primary means\n242:   by which the namespaces in each module are kept separate.\n243: \n244: Each vertex in the graph is evaluated, in an order that guarantees that the\n245: \"happens after\" edges will be respected. If possible, the graph walk algorithm\n246: will evaluate multiple vertices concurrently. Vertex evaluation code must\n247: therefore make careful use of concurrency primitives such as mutexes in order\n248: to coordinate access to shared objects such as the `states.State` object.\n249: In most cases, we use the helper wrapper\n250: [`states.SyncState`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/states#SyncState)\n251: to safely implement concurrent reads and writes from the shared state.\n252: \n253: ## Vertex Evaluation\n254: \n255: The action taken for each vertex during the graph walk is called\n256: _execution_. Execution runs a sequence of arbitrary actions that make sense\n257: for a particular vertex type.\n258: \n259: For example, evaluation of a vertex representing a resource instance during\n260: a plan operation would include the following high-level steps:\n261: \n262: * Retrieve the resource's associated provider from the `EvalContext`. This\n263:   should already be initialized earlier by the provider's own graph vertex,\n264:   due to the \"happens after\" edge between the resource node and the provider\n265:   node.\n266: \n267: * Retrieve from the state the portion relevant to the specific resource\n268:   instance being evaluated.\n269: \n270: * Evaluate the attribute expressions given for the resource in configuration.\n271:   This often involves retrieving the state of _other_ resource instances so\n272:   that their values can be copied or transformed into the current instance's\n273:   attributes, which is coordinated by the `EvalContext`.\n274: \n275: * Pass the current instance state and the resource configuration to the\n276:   provider, asking the provider to produce an _instance diff_ representing the\n277:   differences between the state and the configuration.\n278: \n279: * Save the instance diff as part of the plan that is being constructed by\n280:   this operation.\n281: \n282: Each execution step for a vertex is an implementation of\n283: [`terraform.Execute`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#Execute).\n284: As with graph transforms, the behavior of these implementations varies widely:\n285: whereas graph transforms can take any action against the graph, an `Execute`\n286: implementation can take any action against the `EvalContext`.\n287: \n288: The implementation of `terraform.EvalContext` used in real processing\n289: (as opposed to testing) is\n290: [`terraform.BuiltinEvalContext`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#BuiltinEvalContext).\n291: It provides coordinated access to plugins, the current state, and the current\n292: plan via the `EvalContext` interface methods.\n293: \n294: In order to be executed, a vertex must implement\n295: [`terraform.GraphNodeExecutable`](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#GraphNodeExecutable),\n296: which has a single `Execute` method that handles. There are numerous `Execute`\n297: implementations with different behaviors, but some prominent examples are:\n298: \n299: * [NodePlannableResource.Execute](https://pkg.go.dev/github.com/hashicorp/terraform/internal/terraform#NodePlannableResourceInstance.Execute), which handles the `plan` operation.\n300: \n301: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/terraform--v1.9.0@96263511dba0a473b0636be30c58ee6c489e47ff/-/blob/docs/architecture.md%3FL1-300"}}}
